					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Informal classification of marine mammals, closely related to whales and porpoises</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Dolphin_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Dolphin (disambiguation)">Dolphin (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">


</p>

<p>A <b>dolphin</b> is any one of the 40 <a href="/wiki/Extant_species" class="mw-redirect" title="Extant species">extant species</a> of <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_mammal" title="Aquatic mammal">aquatic mammal</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Cetacean" title="Cetacean">cetacean</a> <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">families</a> <a href="/wiki/Delphinidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Delphinidae">Delphinidae</a> (the oceanic dolphins), <a href="/wiki/Platanistidae" title="Platanistidae">Platanistidae</a> (the Indian river dolphins), <a href="/wiki/Iniidae" title="Iniidae">Iniidae</a> (the New World river dolphins), <a href="/wiki/Pontoporiidae" title="Pontoporiidae">Pontoporiidae</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Brackish" class="mw-redirect" title="Brackish">brackish</a> dolphins), and the probably <a href="/wiki/Extinct" class="mw-redirect" title="Extinct">extinct</a> <a href="/wiki/Lipotidae" title="Lipotidae">Lipotidae</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Baiji" title="Baiji">baiji</a></i> or Chinese river dolphin). All these families belong to the <a href="/wiki/Parvorder" class="mw-redirect" title="Parvorder">parvorder</a> Odontoceti, i.e., <a href="/wiki/Toothed_whale" title="Toothed whale">toothed whales</a>, which also include the closely related families <a href="/wiki/Monodontidae" title="Monodontidae">Monodontidae</a> (<a href="/wiki/Beluga_whale" title="Beluga whale">beluga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Narwhal" title="Narwhal">narwhal</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Phocoenidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Phocoenidae">Phocoenidae</a> (<a href="/wiki/Porpoise" title="Porpoise">porpoises</a>), as well as the more distant families <a href="/wiki/Physeteroidea" title="Physeteroidea">Physeteroidea</a> (sperm whales) and <a href="/wiki/Ziphiidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Ziphiidae">Ziphiidae</a> (beaked whales).
</p><p>Dolphins range in sizes from the man-sized 1.7-metre-long (5&#160;ft 7&#160;in) and 50-kilogram (110-pound) <a href="/wiki/Maui%27s_dolphin" class="mw-redirect" title="Maui&#39;s dolphin">Maui's dolphin</a>; to the 9.5&#160;m (31&#160;ft) and 10-tonne (11-short-ton) <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predator</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orca</a>. Various species of dolphins exhibit <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a> where the males are larger than females. They have <a href="/wiki/Streamlined" class="mw-redirect" title="Streamlined">streamlined</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>-like bodies with the two <a href="/wiki/Forelimb" title="Forelimb">forelimbs</a> <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolving</a> into <a href="/wiki/Flipper_(anatomy)" title="Flipper (anatomy)">flippers</a>, complete loss of <a href="/wiki/Hindlimb" title="Hindlimb">hindlimbs</a> (with only <a href="/wiki/Vestigiality" title="Vestigiality">vestigial</a> <a href="/wiki/Pelvic_skeleton" class="mw-redirect" title="Pelvic skeleton">pelvic skeleton</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tail</a> forming a horizontal <a href="/wiki/Fluke_(tail)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fluke (tail)">fluke</a>. Though not quite as flexible as the <a href="/wiki/Semiaquatic" title="Semiaquatic">semiaquatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a>, they are faster and more agile in water; some dolphins can briefly travel at speeds of 29 kilometres per hour (18&#160;mph) or leap about 9 metres (30&#160;ft).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dolphins use their conical teeth to capture fast-moving <a href="/wiki/Prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Prey">prey</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">forage fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coleoid" class="mw-redirect" title="Coleoid">coleoids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimps</a>. They have well-developed hearing which is adapted for both air and water; so well developed that some can survive even if they are blind.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Some species are well adapted for diving to great depths. Like other aquatic mammals, they have a thick layer of <a href="/wiki/Subcutaneous_tissue" title="Subcutaneous tissue">subcutaneous</a> <a href="/wiki/Adipose_tissue" title="Adipose tissue">adipose tissue</a>, i.e., <a href="/wiki/Blubber" title="Blubber">blubber</a>, to keep the body warm in the cold water.
</p><p>Dolphins are widespread, mostly in <a href="/wiki/Pelagic" class="mw-redirect" title="Pelagic">pelagic</a> <a href="/wiki/Marine_environment" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine environment">marine environments</a> but some thrive in <a href="/wiki/Brackish" class="mw-redirect" title="Brackish">brackish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freshwater" class="mw-redirect" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a> <a href="/wiki/Waterbody" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterbody">bodies</a>. Most species prefer the warm waters of the <a href="/wiki/Tropic" class="mw-redirect" title="Tropic">tropic</a> zones, but some, such as the <a href="/wiki/Right_whale_dolphin" title="Right whale dolphin">right whale dolphin</a>, prefer colder climates. Like most <a href="/wiki/Nekton" title="Nekton">nektonic</a> animals, dolphins are <a href="/wiki/Carnivorous" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnivorous">carnivorous</a>, feed largely <a href="/wiki/Piscivore" title="Piscivore">on fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, but a few large-bodied dolphins, such as the <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orca</a>, prey upon larger marine animals such as seals, other dolphins and porpoises, large bluewater fish such as <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billfish" title="Billfish">billfish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bluefin_tuna" title="Bluefin tuna">bluefin tuna</a>, and sometimes even <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabirds</a>. Male dolphins typically mate with multiple females every year, but females only mate every two to three years. Calves are typically born in the spring and summer months and females bear all the responsibility for raising them. Mothers of some species fast and nurse their young for a relatively long period of time. 
</p><p>Dolphins are highly social animals living in complex "fission-fusion" societies, forming fluid groups (i.e., <i>pods</i>) that constantly change in size and composition. Like other <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whales</a>, dolphins produce a <a href="/wiki/Whale_vocalization" title="Whale vocalization">variety of vocalizations</a>, usually in the form of high-pitched clicks and whistles, and are very keen with <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocation</a>.
</p><p>While relatively uncommon compared to <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">whaling</a>, dolphins are sometimes hunted in places such as <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and parts of <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> (where it is referred to as <i><span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">chancho marino</i></span></i>, meaning "sea pork", in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>), in an activity known as <a href="/wiki/Dolphin_drive_hunting" title="Dolphin drive hunting">dolphin drive hunting</a>. Besides drive hunting, they also face threats from <a href="/wiki/Bycatch" title="Bycatch">bycatch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghost_net" title="Ghost net">ghost net</a> entanglement, <a href="/wiki/Habitat_loss" class="mw-redirect" title="Habitat loss">habitat loss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">marine pollution</a>. Due to their grace and <a href="/wiki/Cetacean_intelligence" title="Cetacean intelligence">high intelligence</a>, dolphins feature prominently in various cultures worldwide, such as in <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a> or <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a>. Dolphins are sometimes kept in <a href="/wiki/Animals_in_captivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Animals in captivity">captivity</a> within <a href="/wiki/Dolphinarium" title="Dolphinarium">dolphinariums</a> for research, conservation or to be trained to perform tricks in <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal_park" title="Marine mammal park">marine mammal parks</a>; the most common dolphin species in captivity is the <a href="/wiki/Bottlenose_dolphin" title="Bottlenose dolphin">bottlenose dolphin</a>, while there are also around 60 <a href="/wiki/Captive_orca" class="mw-redirect" title="Captive orca">orcas in captivity</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2></div>
<p>The name is originally from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">δελφίς</span></span> (<i>delphís</i>), "dolphin",<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Liddel&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liddel-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which was related to the Greek <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">δελφύς</span></span> (<i>delphus</i>), "womb".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Liddel&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liddel-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The animal's name can therefore be interpreted as meaning "a 'fish' with a womb".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The name was transmitted via the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>delphinus</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (the <a href="/wiki/Romanization" title="Romanization">romanization</a> of the later Greek δελφῖνος – <i>delphinos</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Liddel&#95;4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liddel-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>), which in <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> became <span title="Medieval Latin-language text"><i lang="la">dolfinus</i></span> and in <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i>daulphin</i>, which reintroduced the spelling <i>ph</i> into the word <i>dolphin</i>. The term <i>mereswine</i> ("sea pig") is also used.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-c959&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-c959-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-l962&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-l962-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Definition"></span>
The term <i>dolphin</i> can be used to refer to most species in the family <a href="/wiki/Delphinidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Delphinidae">Delphinidae</a> (oceanic dolphins) and the river dolphin families of <a href="/wiki/Iniidae" title="Iniidae">Iniidae</a> (South American river dolphins), Pontoporiidae (<a href="/wiki/La_Plata_dolphin" title="La Plata dolphin">La Plata dolphin</a>), <a href="/wiki/Lipotidae" title="Lipotidae">Lipotidae</a> (Yangtze river dolphin) and <a href="/wiki/Platanistidae" title="Platanistidae">Platanistidae</a> (Ganges river dolphin and Indus river dolphin).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Walker2007&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker2007-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/Mahi-mahi" title="Mahi-mahi">mahi-mahi</a> fish is called the dolphinfish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In common usage, the term <i>whale</i> is used only for the larger cetacean species,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Leatherwood1988&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leatherwood1988-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while the smaller ones with a beaked or longer nose are considered dolphins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hirschi2002&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirschi2002-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The name <i>dolphin</i> is used casually as a synonym for <a href="/wiki/Bottlenose_dolphin" title="Bottlenose dolphin">bottlenose dolphin</a>, the most common and familiar species of dolphin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NowacekNowacek2006&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NowacekNowacek2006-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There are six species of dolphins commonly thought of as whales, collectively known as <a href="/wiki/Blackfish" title="Blackfish">blackfish</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orca</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Melon-headed_whale" title="Melon-headed whale">melon-headed whale</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_killer_whale" title="Pygmy killer whale">pygmy killer whale</a>, the <a href="/wiki/False_killer_whale" title="False killer whale">false killer whale</a>, and the two species of <a href="/wiki/Pilot_whale" title="Pilot whale">pilot whales</a>, all of which are classified under the family Delphinidae and qualify as dolphins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although the terms <i>dolphin</i> and <i>porpoise</i> are sometimes used interchangeably, <i>porpoise</i> usually refers to the Phocoenidae family, which have a shorter beak and spade-shaped teeth and differ in their behavior.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NowacekNowacek2006&#95;14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NowacekNowacek2006-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A group of dolphins is called a <i>school</i> or a <i>pod</i>. Male dolphins are called <i>bulls</i>, females are called <i>cows</i> and young dolphins are called <i>calves</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Evolution">Evolution<span class="anchor" id="Evolution&#95;and&#95;anatomy"></span></h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_cetaceans" title="Evolution of cetaceans">Evolution of cetaceans</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ichthyosaur_vs_dolphin.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Ichthyosaur_vs_dolphin.svg/250px-Ichthyosaur_vs_dolphin.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Ichthyosaur_vs_dolphin.svg/500px-Ichthyosaur_vs_dolphin.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a><figcaption>Dolphins display <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a> with fish and <a href="/wiki/Ichthyosauria" title="Ichthyosauria">aquatic reptiles</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Dolphins are descendants of land-dwelling mammals of the <a href="/wiki/Artiodactyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Artiodactyl">artiodactyl</a> <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> (even-toed ungulates). They are related to the <i><a href="/wiki/Indohyus" title="Indohyus">Indohyus</a></i>, an extinct <a href="/wiki/Chevrotain" title="Chevrotain">chevrotain</a>-like ungulate, from which they split approximately 48 million years ago.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-science&#95;news&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science_news-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ancestors&#95;Tale&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ancestors_Tale-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The primitive cetaceans, or <a href="/wiki/Archaeocetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaeocetes">archaeocetes</a>, first took to the sea approximately 49 million years ago and became fully aquatic by 5–10&#160;million years later.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-radiations&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-radiations-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Archaeoceti" title="Archaeoceti">Archaeoceti</a> is a parvorder comprising ancient whales. These ancient whales are the predecessors of modern whales, stretching back to their first ancestor that spent their lives near (rarely in) the water. Likewise, the archaeocetes can be anywhere from near fully terrestrial, to semi-aquatic to fully aquatic, but what defines an archaeocete is the presence of visible legs or asymmetrical teeth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Their features became adapted for living in the <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">marine environment</a>. Major anatomical changes include the hearing set-up that channeled vibrations from the jaw to the earbone which occurred with <i><a href="/wiki/Ambulocetus" title="Ambulocetus">Ambulocetus</a></i> 49 million years ago, a <a href="/wiki/Streamline_(fluid_dynamics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Streamline (fluid dynamics)">streamlining</a> of the body and the growth of flukes on the tail which occurred around 43 million years ago with <i><a href="/wiki/Protocetus" title="Protocetus">Protocetus</a></i>, the migration of the nasal openings toward the top of the <a href="/wiki/Cranium" class="mw-redirect" title="Cranium">cranium</a> and the modification of the forelimbs into flippers which occurred with <i><a href="/wiki/Basilosaurus" title="Basilosaurus">Basilosaurus</a></i> 35 million years ago, and the shrinking and eventual disappearance of the hind limbs which took place with the first odontocetes and mysticetes 34&#160;million years ago.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The modern dolphin <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeleton</a> has two small, rod-shaped pelvic bones thought to be <a href="/wiki/Vestigial" class="mw-redirect" title="Vestigial">vestigial</a> hind limbs. In October 2006, an unusual bottlenose dolphin was captured in Japan; it had small <a href="/wiki/Fin" title="Fin">fins</a> on each side of its genital slit, which scientists believe to be an unusually pronounced development of these vestigial hind limbs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Today, the closest living relatives of cetaceans are the <a href="/wiki/Hippopotamus" title="Hippopotamus">hippopotamuses</a>; these share a semi-aquatic ancestor that branched off from other artiodactyls some 60 million years ago.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Around 40 million years ago, a common ancestor between the two branched off into cetacea and <a href="/wiki/Anthracotheres" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthracotheres">anthracotheres</a>; anthracotheres became extinct at the end of the Pleistocene two-and-a-half million years ago, eventually leaving only one surviving lineage: the two species of hippo.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Anatomy">Anatomy</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dolphin_Anatomy.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dolphin_Anatomy.svg/500px-Dolphin_Anatomy.svg.png" decoding="async" width="340" height="204" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dolphin_Anatomy.svg/960px-Dolphin_Anatomy.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2493" data-file-height="1493" /></a><figcaption>The anatomy of a dolphin showing its skeleton, major organs, tail and body shape.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Dolphins have torpedo-shaped bodies with generally non-flexible necks, limbs modified into flippers, a tail fin, and bulbous heads. Dolphin skulls have small eye orbits, long snouts, and eyes placed on the sides of its head; they lack external ear flaps. Dolphins range in size from the 1.7&#160;m (5&#160;ft 7&#160;in) long and 50&#160;kg (110&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Maui%27s_dolphin" class="mw-redirect" title="Maui&#39;s dolphin">Maui's dolphin</a> to the 9.5&#160;m (31&#160;ft 2&#160;in) and 10&#160;t (11 short tons) <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orca</a>. Overall, they tend to be dwarfed by other <a href="/wiki/Cetartiodactyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Cetartiodactyl">Cetartiodactyls</a>. Several species have female-biased sexual dimorphism, with the females being larger than the males.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cetacean&#95;anatomy&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cetacean_anatomy-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dolphins have conical teeth, as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Porpoise" title="Porpoise">porpoises</a>' spade-shaped teeth. These conical teeth are used to catch swift prey such as fish, squid or large mammals, such as seals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cetacean&#95;anatomy&#95;32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cetacean_anatomy-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Breathing involves expelling stale air from their <a href="/wiki/Blowhole_(anatomy)" title="Blowhole (anatomy)">blowhole</a>, in an upward blast, which may be visible in cold air, followed by inhaling fresh air into the lungs. Dolphins have rather small, unidentifiable spouts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cetacean&#95;anatomy&#95;32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cetacean_anatomy-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>All dolphins have a thick layer of <a href="/wiki/Blubber" title="Blubber">blubber</a>, thickness varying on climate. This blubber can help with buoyancy, protection to some extent as predators would have a hard time getting through a thick layer of fat, and energy for leaner times; the primary usage for blubber is insulation from the harsh climate. Calves, generally, are born with a thin layer of blubber, which develops at different paces depending on the habitat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cetacean&#95;anatomy&#95;32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cetacean_anatomy-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dolphins&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dolphins-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dolphins have a two-chambered<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Stevens1995&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevens1995-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or three-chambered<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Biancani2022&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biancani2022-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> stomach that is similar in cellular structure to that of terrestrial carnivores. They have <a href="/wiki/Fundic_stomach" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundic stomach">fundic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyloric_stomach" class="mw-redirect" title="Pyloric stomach">pyloric</a> chambers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Stevens1995&#95;35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevens1995-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dolphins' reproductive organs are located inside the body, with genital slits on the ventral (belly) side. Males have two slits, one concealing the <a href="/wiki/Penis" title="Penis">penis</a> and one further behind for the <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-PerrinWursig2009&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PerrinWursig2009-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Females have one genital slit, housing the <a href="/wiki/Vagina" title="Vagina">vagina</a> and the anus, with a <a href="/wiki/Mammary_gland" title="Mammary gland">mammary slit</a> on either side.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Integumentary_system">Integumentary system</h3></div>
<p>The integumentary system is an organ system mostly consisting of skin, hair, nails and endocrine glands. The skin of dolphins is specialized to satisfy specific requirements, including protection, fat storage, heat regulation, and sensory perception. The skin of a dolphin is made up of two parts: the epidermis and the blubber, which consists of two layers including the dermis and subcutis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cozzi-2017&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cozzi-2017-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The dolphin's skin is known to have a smooth rubber texture and is without hair and glands, except mammary glands. At birth, a newborn dolphin has hairs lined up in a single band on both sides of the rostrum, which is their jaw, and usually has a total length of 16–17&#160;cm .<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cozzi-2017&#95;41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cozzi-2017-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The epidermis is characterized by the lack of keratin and by a prominent intertwine of epidermal <a href="/wiki/Rete_pegs" title="Rete pegs">rete pegs</a> and long dermal papillae.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cozzi-2017&#95;41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cozzi-2017-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The epidermal rete pegs are the epithelial extensions that project into the underlying connective tissue in both skin and mucous membranes. The dermal papillae are finger-like projections that help adhesion between the epidermal and dermal layers, as well as providing a larger surface area to nourish the epidermal layer.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The thickness of a dolphin's epidermis varies, depending on species and age.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Blubber">Blubber</h4></div>
<p>Blubber is found within the dermis and subcutis layer. The dermis blends gradually with the adipose layer, which is known as fat, because the fat may extend up to the epidermis border and collagen fiber bundles extend throughout the whole subcutaneous blubber which is fat found under the skin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cozzi-2017&#95;41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cozzi-2017-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The thickness of the subcutaneous blubber or fat depends on the dolphin's health, development, location, reproductive state, and how well it feeds. This fat is thickest on the dolphin's back and belly. Most of the dolphin's body fat is accumulated in a thick layer of blubber. Blubber differs from fat in that, in addition to fat cells, it contains a fibrous network of connective tissue.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The blubber functions to streamline the body and to form specialized locomotor structures such as the dorsal fin, propulsive fluke blades and caudal keels.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cozzi-2017&#95;41-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cozzi-2017-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There are many nerve endings that resemble small, onion-like configurations that are present in the superficial portion of the dermis. Mechanoreceptors are found within the interlocks of the epidermis with dermal ridges. There are nerve fibers in the dermis that extend to the epidermis. These nerve endings are known to be highly <a href="/wiki/Proprioception" title="Proprioception">proprioceptive</a>, which explains sensory perception.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cozzi-2017&#95;41-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cozzi-2017-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Proprioception, which is also known as kinesthesia, is the body's ability to sense its location, movements and actions. Dolphins are sensitive to vibrations and small pressure changes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kremers&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kremers-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Blood vessels and nerve endings can be found within the dermis. There is a plexus of parallel running arteries and veins in the dorsal fin, fluke, and flippers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cozzi-2017&#95;41-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cozzi-2017-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The blubber manipulates the blood vessels to help the dolphin stay warm. When the temperature drops, the blubber constricts the blood vessels to reduce blood flow in the dolphin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This allows the dolphin to spend less energy heating its own body, ultimately keeping the animal warmer without burning energy as quick. In order to release heat, the heat must pass the blubber layer. There are thermal windows that lack blubber, are not fully insulated and are somewhat thin and highly vascularized, including the dorsal fin, flukes, and flippers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-WDC-2012&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WDC-2012-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These thermal windows are a good way for dolphins to get rid of excess heat if overheating. Additionally in order to conserve heat, dolphins use countercurrent heat exchange. Blood flows in different directions in order for heat to transfer across membranes. Heat from warm blood leaving the heart will heat up the cold blood that is headed back to the heart from the extremities, meaning that the heart always has warm blood and it decreases the heat lost to the water in those thermal windows.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-WDC-2012&#95;46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WDC-2012-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm/250px--Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="250" height="141" data-durationhint="37" data-mwtitle="Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f0/Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm/Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" data-height="480" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f0/Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm/Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm.1080p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="1080p.vp9.webm" data-width="1920" data-height="1080" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9&quot;" data-width="1920" data-height="1080" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f0/Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm/Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm.144p.mjpeg.mov" type="video/quicktime" data-transcodekey="144p.mjpeg.mov" data-width="256" data-height="144" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f0/Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm/Bottlenose_dolphin_-_kanagawa_-_2025_March_21.webm.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" /></video></span><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Bottlenose_dolphin" title="Bottlenose dolphin">Bottlenose dolphins</a> swimming and jumping in captivity, 2025</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Locomotion">Locomotion</h3></div>
<p>Dolphins have two pectoral flippers,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> each containing four digits, a boneless <a href="/wiki/Dorsal_fin" title="Dorsal fin">dorsal fin</a> for stability, and a fluke for propulsion. Although dolphins do not possess external hind limbs, some possess discrete rudimentary appendages, which may contain feet and digits. Orcas are fast swimmers in comparison to seals which typically cruise at 9–28&#160;km/h (5.6–17.4&#160;mph); the orca, in comparison, can travel at speeds up to 55.5&#160;km/h (34.5&#160;mph).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> A study of a Pacific white-sided dolphin in an aquarium found fast burst acceleration, with the individual being able with 5 strokes (2.5 fluke beats) to go from 5.0 m s-1 to 8.7 m s-1 in 0.7 seconds.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tanaka&#95;Li&#95;Uchida&#95;Nakamura&#95;2019&#95;p.&#95;e0210860&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tanaka_Li_Uchida_Nakamura_2019_p._e0210860-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The fusing of the neck vertebrae, while increasing stability when swimming at high speeds, decreases flexibility, which means most dolphins are unable to turn their heads.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/River_dolphin" title="River dolphin">River dolphins</a> have non-fused neck vertebrae and can turn their heads up to 90°.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dolphins swim by moving their fluke and rear body vertically, while their flippers are mainly used for steering. Some species <a href="/wiki/Cetacean_surfacing_behaviour#Porpoising" title="Cetacean surfacing behaviour">porpoise</a> out of the water, which allows them to travel faster. Their skeletal anatomy allows them to be fast swimmers. All species have a <a href="/wiki/Dorsal_fin" title="Dorsal fin">dorsal fin</a> to prevent themselves from involuntarily spinning in the water.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cetacean&#95;anatomy&#95;32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cetacean_anatomy-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dolphins&#95;34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dolphins-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some dolphins are adapted for diving to great depths. In addition to their streamlined bodies, some can selectively slow their <a href="/wiki/Heart_rate" title="Heart rate">heart rate</a> to conserve oxygen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-phys-dolphins&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-phys-dolphins-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some can also re-route blood from tissue tolerant of water pressure to the heart, brain and other organs. Their <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a> store oxygen in body tissues, and they have twice as much myoglobin as hemoglobin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Senses">Senses</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Delfinekko.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Delfinekko.gif/250px-Delfinekko.gif" decoding="async" width="250" height="96" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/Delfinekko.gif 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="154" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Biosonar" class="mw-redirect" title="Biosonar">Biosonar</a> by cetaceans</figcaption></figure>
<p><span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Toothed_whale_sound_production.svg" class="mw-file-description" title="Diagram illustrating sound generation, propagation, and reception in a toothed whale. Outgoing sounds are in cyan and incoming ones are in green"><img alt="Diagram illustrating sound generation, propagation, and reception in a toothed whale. Outgoing sounds are in cyan and incoming ones are in green" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Toothed_whale_sound_production.svg/960px-Toothed_whale_sound_production.svg.png" decoding="async" width="512" height="260" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Toothed_whale_sound_production.svg/1280px-Toothed_whale_sound_production.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="260" /></a></span>
</p><p>A dolphin ear has specific adaptations to the <a href="/wiki/Marine_(ocean)" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine (ocean)">marine</a> environment. In humans, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_ear" title="Middle ear">middle ear</a> works as an impedance equalizer between the outside air's low <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_impedance" title="Acoustic impedance">impedance</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cochlea" title="Cochlea">cochlear</a> fluid's high impedance. In dolphins, and other marine mammals, there is no great difference between the outer and inner environments. Instead of sound passing through the outer ear to the middle ear, dolphins receive sound through the throat, from which it passes through a low-impedance fat-filled cavity to the inner ear. The ear is acoustically isolated from the skull by air-filled sinus pockets, which allow for greater directional hearing underwater.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Dolphins generate sounds independently of respiration using recycled air that passes through air sacs and phonic (alternatively monkey) lips. Integral to the lips are oil-filled organs called dorsal bursae that have been suggested to be homologous to the sperm whale's spermaceti organ.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-n010&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-n010-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> High-frequency clicks pass through the sound-modifying organs of the extramandibular fat body, intramandibular fat body and the <a href="/wiki/Melon_(cetacean)" title="Melon (cetacean)">melon</a>. This melon consists of fat, and the skull of any such creature containing a melon will have a large depression. This allows dolphins to use <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocation</a> for orientation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cetacean&#95;anatomy&#95;32-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cetacean_anatomy-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dolphin&#95;senses&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dolphin_senses-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Thewissen-Hearing&#95;58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thewissen-Hearing-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Though most dolphins do not have hair, they do have <a href="/wiki/Hair_follicle" title="Hair follicle">hair follicles</a> that may perform some sensory function.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Beyond locating an object, <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocation</a> also provides the animal with an idea on an object's shape and size, though how exactly this works is not yet understood.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The small hairs on the rostrum of the <a href="/wiki/Boto" title="Boto">boto</a> (river dolphins of South America) are believed to function as a <a href="/wiki/Somatosensory_system" title="Somatosensory system">tactile sense</a>, possibly to compensate for the boto's poor eyesight.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A dolphin eye is relatively small for its size, yet they do retain a good degree of eyesight. As well as this, the eyes of a dolphin are placed on the sides of its head, so their vision consists of two fields, rather than a binocular view like humans have. When dolphins surface, their lens and cornea correct the nearsightedness that results from the water's refraction of light. Their eyes contain both <a href="/wiki/Rod_cell" title="Rod cell">rod</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cone_cell" title="Cone cell">cone</a> cells, meaning they can see in both dim and bright light, but they have far more rod cells than they do cone cells. They lack short wavelength sensitive visual pigments in their cone cells, indicating a more limited capacity for color vision than most mammals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most dolphins have slightly flattened eyeballs, enlarged pupils (which shrink as they surface to prevent damage), slightly flattened corneas and a <i><a href="/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum" title="Tapetum lucidum">tapetum lucidum</a></i> (eye tissue behind the retina); these adaptations allow for large amounts of light to pass through the eye and, therefore, a very clear image of the surrounding area. They also have glands on the eyelids and <a href="/wiki/Corneal_epithelium" title="Corneal epithelium">outer corneal layer</a> that act as protection for the cornea.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dolphin&#95;senses&#95;57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dolphin_senses-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_lobes" class="mw-redirect" title="Olfactory lobes">olfactory lobes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_nerve" title="Olfactory nerve">nerve</a> are absent in dolphins, suggesting that they have no sense of smell.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dolphin&#95;senses&#95;57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dolphin_senses-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dolphins are not thought to have a good sense of taste, as their taste buds are atrophied or missing altogether. Some have preferences for different kinds of fish, indicating some ability to taste.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dolphin&#95;senses&#95;57-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dolphin_senses-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Intelligence">Intelligence</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cetacean_intelligence" title="Cetacean intelligence">Cetacean intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cetacean_surfacing_behaviour" title="Cetacean surfacing behaviour">Cetacean surfacing behaviour</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dolphin_Encounter-9563.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Dolphin_Encounter-9563.jpg/250px-Dolphin_Encounter-9563.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Dolphin_Encounter-9563.jpg/500px-Dolphin_Encounter-9563.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1728" /></a><figcaption>The face of a <a href="/wiki/Common_bottlenose_dolphin" title="Common bottlenose dolphin">common bottlenose dolphin</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Dolphins are known to teach, learn, cooperate, scheme, and grieve.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wwwu&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wwwu-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The neocortex of many species is home to elongated <a href="/wiki/Spindle_neurons" class="mw-redirect" title="Spindle neurons">spindle neurons</a> that, prior to 2007, were known only in hominids.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In humans, these cells are involved in social conduct, emotions, judgment, and theory of mind.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cetacean spindle neurons are found in areas of the brain that are analogous to where they are found in humans, suggesting that they perform a similar function.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Brain_size" title="Brain size">Brain size</a> was previously considered a major indicator of the intelligence of an animal. Since most of the brain is used for maintaining bodily functions, greater ratios of brain to body mass may increase the amount of brain mass available for more complex cognitive tasks. <a href="/wiki/Allometric" class="mw-redirect" title="Allometric">Allometric</a> analysis indicates that mammalian brain size scales at approximately the two-thirds or three-fourths exponent of the body mass. <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Comparison of a particular animal's brain size with the expected brain size based on such allometric analysis provides an <a href="/wiki/Encephalization_quotient" title="Encephalization quotient">encephalization quotient</a> that can be used as another indication of animal intelligence. <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">Orcas</a> have the second largest brain mass of any animal on earth, next to the <a href="/wiki/Sperm_whale" title="Sperm whale">sperm whale</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Brain_to_body_mass_ratio" class="mw-redirect" title="Brain to body mass ratio">brain to body mass ratio</a> in some is second only to humans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Self-awareness" title="Self-awareness">Self-awareness</a> is seen, by some, to be a sign of highly developed, abstract thinking. Self-awareness, though not well-defined scientifically, is believed to be the precursor to more advanced processes like <a href="/wiki/Metacognition" title="Metacognition">meta-cognitive reasoning</a> (thinking about thinking) that are typical of humans. Research in this field has suggested that cetaceans, among others, possess self-awareness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
The most widely used test for self-awareness in animals is the <a href="/wiki/Mirror_test" title="Mirror test">mirror test</a> in which a mirror is introduced to an animal, and the animal is then marked with a temporary dye. If the animal then goes to the mirror in order to view the mark, it has exhibited strong evidence of self-awareness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mirror&#95;test&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mirror_test-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some disagree with these findings, arguing that the results of these tests are open to human interpretation and susceptible to the <a href="/wiki/Clever_Hans" title="Clever Hans">Clever Hans</a> effect. This test is much less definitive than when used for primates, because primates can touch the mark or the mirror, while cetaceans cannot, making their alleged self-recognition behavior less certain. Skeptics argue that behaviors that are said to identify self-awareness resemble existing social behaviors, and so researchers could be misinterpreting self-awareness for social responses to another individual. The researchers counter-argue that the behaviors shown are evidence of self-awareness, as they are very different from normal responses to another individual. Whereas apes can merely touch the mark on themselves with their fingers, cetaceans show less definitive behavior of self-awareness; they can only twist and turn themselves to observe the mark.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mirror&#95;test&#95;73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mirror_test-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1995, Marten and Psarakos used television to test dolphin self-awareness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They showed dolphins real-time video of themselves, video of another dolphin and recorded footage. They concluded that their evidence suggested self-awareness rather than social behavior. While this particular study has not been repeated since then, dolphins have since passed the mirror test.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mirror&#95;test&#95;73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mirror_test-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some researchers have argued that evidence for self-awareness has not been convincingly demonstrated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Behavior">Behavior</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dolphins_gesture_language.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Dolphins_gesture_language.jpg/250px-Dolphins_gesture_language.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Dolphins_gesture_language.jpg/500px-Dolphins_gesture_language.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>A pod of <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific_bottlenose_dolphin" title="Indo-Pacific bottlenose dolphin">Indo-Pacific bottlenose dolphins</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a></figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Socialization">Socialization</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dolphinsurfresize.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Dolphinsurfresize.jpg/250px-Dolphinsurfresize.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="333" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Dolphinsurfresize.jpg/500px-Dolphinsurfresize.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="800" /></a><figcaption>Dolphins surfing at <a href="/wiki/Snapper_Rocks" title="Snapper Rocks">Snapper Rocks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a>, Australia</figcaption></figure>
<p>Dolphins are highly social animals, often living in pods of up to a dozen individuals, though pod sizes and structures vary greatly between species and locations. In places with a high abundance of food, pods can merge temporarily, forming a <i>superpod</i>; such groupings may exceed 1,000 dolphins. Membership in pods is not rigid; interchange is common. They establish strong social bonds, and will stay with injured or ill members, helping them to breathe by bringing them to the surface if needed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Altruism" title="Altruism">altruism</a> does not appear to be limited to their own species. The dolphin <i><a href="/wiki/Moko_(dolphin)" title="Moko (dolphin)">Moko</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> has been observed guiding a female <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_sperm_whale" title="Pygmy sperm whale">pygmy sperm whale</a> together with her calf out of shallow water where they had stranded several times.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They have also been seen protecting swimmers from sharks by swimming circles around the swimmers<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or charging the sharks to make them go away.
</p><p>Dolphins communicate using a variety of clicks, whistle-like sounds and other vocalizations. Dolphins also use <a href="/wiki/Nonverbal_communication" title="Nonverbal communication">nonverbal communication</a> by means of touch and <a href="/wiki/Posture_(psychology)" title="Posture (psychology)">posturing</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dolphins also display culture, something long believed to be unique to humans (and possibly other <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primate</a> species). In May 2005, a discovery in Australia found <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific_bottlenose_dolphin" title="Indo-Pacific bottlenose dolphin">Indo-Pacific bottlenose dolphins</a> (<i>Tursiops aduncus</i>) teaching their young to use tools. They cover their <a href="/wiki/Snout" title="Snout">snouts</a> with <a href="/wiki/Sea_sponge" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea sponge">sponges</a> to protect them while foraging. This knowledge is mostly transferred by mothers to daughters, unlike <a href="/wiki/Simian" title="Simian">simian</a> primates, where knowledge is generally passed on to both sexes. Using sponges as mouth protection is a learned behavior.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another learned behavior was discovered among river dolphins in Brazil, where some male dolphins use weeds and sticks as part of a sexual display.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Forms of care-giving between fellows and even for members of different species<sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Moko_(dolphin)" title="Moko (dolphin)">Moko (dolphin)</a>) are recorded in various species –  such as trying to save weakened fellows<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or female <a href="/wiki/Pilot_whale" title="Pilot whale">pilot whales</a> holding up dead calves for long periods.
</p><p>Dolphins engage in acts of aggression towards each other. The older a male dolphin is, the more likely his body is to be covered with bite scars. Male dolphins can get into disputes over companions and females. Acts of aggression can become so intense that targeted dolphins sometimes go into exile after losing a fight.
</p><p>Male bottlenose dolphins have been known to engage in <a href="/wiki/Infanticide_(zoology)" title="Infanticide (zoology)">infanticide</a>. Dolphins have also been known to kill <a href="/wiki/Porpoises" class="mw-redirect" title="Porpoises">porpoises</a> (porpicide) for reasons which are not fully understood, as porpoises generally do not share the same diet as dolphins and are therefore not competitors for food supplies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Wildlife_Trust" title="Cornwall Wildlife Trust">Cornwall Wildlife Trust</a> records about one such death a year. Possible explanations include misdirected infanticide, misdirected sexual aggression or <a href="/wiki/Play_behaviour" class="mw-redirect" title="Play behaviour">play behaviour</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Reproduction_and_sexuality">Reproduction and sexuality<span class="anchor" id="Reproductive&#95;system"></span></h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bottlenose_dolphin#Reproduction" title="Bottlenose dolphin">Bottlenose dolphin §&#160;Reproduction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dusky_dolphin#Social_behavior_and_reproduction" title="Dusky dolphin">Dusky dolphin §&#160;Social behavior and reproduction</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Short-beaked_common_dolphin#Reproduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Short-beaked common dolphin">Short-beaked common dolphin §&#160;Reproduction</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cmglee_Horniman_dolphin_skin_skeleton.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Cmglee_Horniman_dolphin_skin_skeleton.jpg/250px-Cmglee_Horniman_dolphin_skin_skeleton.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="125" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Cmglee_Horniman_dolphin_skin_skeleton.jpg/500px-Cmglee_Horniman_dolphin_skin_skeleton.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1024" /></a><figcaption>A skin-skeletal preparation.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Dolphin <a href="/wiki/Copulation_(zoology)" title="Copulation (zoology)">copulation</a> happens belly to belly; though many species engage in lengthy <a href="/wiki/Foreplay" title="Foreplay">foreplay</a>, the actual act is usually brief, but may be repeated several times within a short timespan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Silva&#95;88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Silva-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gestation_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Gestation period">gestation period</a> varies with species; for the small <a href="/wiki/Tucuxi_dolphin" class="mw-redirect" title="Tucuxi dolphin">tucuxi dolphin</a>, this period is around 11 to 12 months,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while for the orca, the gestation period is around 17 months.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Typically dolphins give birth to a single calf, which is, unlike most other mammals, born tail first in most cases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Simmonds2007&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simmonds2007-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They usually become sexually active at a young age, even before reaching <a href="/wiki/Sexual_maturity" title="Sexual maturity">sexual maturity</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Silva&#95;88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Silva-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The age of sexual maturity varies by species and sex.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dolphins are known to display <a href="/wiki/Animal_co-opted_sexual_behavior" class="mw-redirect" title="Animal co-opted sexual behavior">non-reproductive sexual behavior</a>, engaging in <a href="/wiki/Animal_sexual_behavior#Autoeroticism_or_masturbation" class="mw-redirect" title="Animal sexual behavior">masturbation</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> stimulation of the genital area of other individuals using the <a href="/wiki/Rostrum_(anatomy)" title="Rostrum (anatomy)">rostrum</a> or flippers, and <a href="/wiki/Homosexual_behavior_in_animals" title="Homosexual behavior in animals">homosexual contact</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Silva&#95;88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Silva-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Various species of dolphin have been known to engage in sexual behavior including copulation with dolphins of other species, and occasionally exhibit <a href="/wiki/Cross-species_sex" class="mw-redirect" title="Cross-species sex">sexual behavior towards other animals</a>, including humans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sexual encounters may be violent, with male bottlenose dolphins sometimes showing aggressive behavior towards both females and other males.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Male dolphins may also work together and attempt to herd females in <a href="/wiki/Estrus#Estrus" class="mw-redirect" title="Estrus">estrus</a>, keeping the females by their side by means of both physical aggression and intimidation, to increase their chances of reproductive success.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Hybridization">Hybridization</h4></div>
<p>In 1933, three hybrid dolphins beached off the Irish coast; they were <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrids</a> between <a href="/wiki/Risso%27s_dolphin" title="Risso&#39;s dolphin">Risso's</a> and bottlenose dolphins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This mating was later repeated in captivity, producing a hybrid calf. In captivity, a bottlenose and a <a href="/wiki/Rough-toothed_dolphin" title="Rough-toothed dolphin">rough-toothed dolphin</a> produced hybrid offspring.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A common-bottlenose hybrid lives at <a href="/wiki/SeaWorld" title="SeaWorld">SeaWorld</a> California.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other dolphin hybrids live in captivity around the world or have been reported in the wild, such as a bottlenose-<a href="/wiki/Atlantic_spotted_dolphin" title="Atlantic spotted dolphin">Atlantic spotted</a> hybrid.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The best known hybrid is the <a href="/wiki/Wholphin" title="Wholphin">wholphin</a>, a false killer whale-bottlenose dolphin hybrid. The wholphin is a fertile hybrid. Two wholphins currently live at the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Life_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea Life Park">Sea Life Park</a> in Hawaii; the first was born in 1985 from a male false killer whale and a female bottlenose. Wholphins have also been observed in the wild.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sleeping">Sleeping</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:%D7%93%D7%95%D7%9C%D7%A4%D7%99%D7%9F_%D7%99%D7%A9%D7%9F.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/%D7%93%D7%95%D7%9C%D7%A4%D7%99%D7%9F_%D7%99%D7%A9%D7%9F.svg/250px-%D7%93%D7%95%D7%9C%D7%A4%D7%99%D7%9F_%D7%99%D7%A9%D7%9F.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="213" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/%D7%93%D7%95%D7%9C%D7%A4%D7%99%D7%9F_%D7%99%D7%A9%D7%9F.svg/500px-%D7%93%D7%95%D7%9C%D7%A4%D7%99%D7%9F_%D7%99%D7%A9%D7%9F.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="468" data-file-height="398" /></a><figcaption>Sleeping dolphin in captivity: a tail kick reflex keeps the dolphin's <a href="/wiki/Blowhole_(anatomy)" title="Blowhole (anatomy)">blowhole</a> above the water.</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sleep_in_animals" title="Sleep in animals">Sleep in animals</a></div>
<p>Generally, dolphins sleep with only one brain <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_hemisphere" title="Cerebral hemisphere">hemisphere</a> in <a href="/wiki/Slow-wave_sleep" title="Slow-wave sleep">slow-wave sleep</a> at a time, thus maintaining enough consciousness to breathe and to watch for possible predators and other threats. Sleep stages earlier in sleep can occur simultaneously in both hemispheres.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mukhametov,&#95;1984&#95;106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mukhametov,_1984-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
In captivity, dolphins seemingly enter a fully asleep state where both eyes are closed and there is no response to mild external stimuli. In this case, respiration is automatic; a tail kick <a href="/wiki/Reflex" title="Reflex">reflex</a> keeps the blowhole above the water if necessary. <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">Anesthetized</a> dolphins initially show a tail kick reflex.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Though a similar state has been observed with wild <a href="/wiki/Sperm_whale" title="Sperm whale">sperm whales</a>, it is not known if dolphins in the wild reach this state.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Indus_river_dolphin" title="Indus river dolphin">Indus river dolphin</a> has a sleep method that is different from that of other dolphin species. Living in water with strong currents and potentially dangerous floating debris, it must swim continuously to avoid injury. As a result, this species sleeps in very short bursts which last between 4 and 60&#160;seconds.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Feeding">Feeding</h3></div>
<p>There are various feeding methods among and within species, some apparently exclusive to a single population. Fish and squid are the main food, but the false killer whale and the orca also feed on other marine mammals. Orcas on occasion also hunt whale species larger than themselves.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FactsDiet&#95;111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FactsDiet-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Different breeds of dolphins vary widely in the number of teeth they possess. The orca usually carries 40–56 teeth while the popular bottlenose dolphin has anywhere from 72 to 116 conical teeth and its smaller cousin the common dolphin has 188–268 teeth: the number of teeth that an individual carries varies widely between within a single species. Hybrids between common and bottlenose bred in captivity had a number of teeth intermediate between that of their parents.
</p><p>One common feeding method is herding, where a pod squeezes a school of fish into a small volume, known as a <a href="/wiki/Bait_ball" title="Bait ball">bait ball</a>. Individual members then take turns plowing through the ball, feeding on the stunned fish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FactsDiet&#95;111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FactsDiet-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Corralling is a method where dolphins chase fish into shallow water to catch them more easily.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FactsDiet&#95;111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FactsDiet-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Orcas and bottlenose dolphins have also been known to drive their prey onto a beach to feed on it, a behaviour known as beach or strand feeding.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some species also whack fish with their flukes, stunning them and sometimes knocking them out of the water.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FactsDiet&#95;111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FactsDiet-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Reports of cooperative human-dolphin fishing date back to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Roman</a> author and <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A modern human-dolphin partnership currently operates in <a href="/wiki/Laguna,_Santa_Catarina" title="Laguna, Santa Catarina">Laguna</a>, Santa Catarina, Brazil. Here, dolphins drive fish towards fishermen waiting along the shore and signal the men to cast their nets. The dolphins' reward is the fish that escape the nets.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
