</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Shark_Bay" title="Shark Bay">Shark Bay</a>, Australia, dolphins catch fish by trapping them in huge <a href="/wiki/Conch" title="Conch">conch shells</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In "shelling", a dolphin brings the shell to the surface and shakes it, so that fish sheltering within fall into the dolphin's mouth. From 2007 to 2018, in 5,278 encounters with dolphins, researchers observed 19 dolphins shelling 42 times. The behavior spreads mainly within generations, rather than being passed from mother to offspring.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Vocalization">Vocalization</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dolphin1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6c/Dolphin1.jpg" decoding="async" width="340" height="256" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="431" data-file-height="324" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Spectrogram" title="Spectrogram">Spectrogram</a> of dolphin vocalizations. Whistles, whines, and clicks are visible as upside down V's, horizontal striations, and vertical lines, respectively.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Dolphins are capable of making a broad range of sounds using nasal airsacs located just below the blowhole. Roughly three categories of sounds can be identified: <a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulated" class="mw-redirect" title="Frequency modulated">frequency modulated</a> whistles, burst-pulsed sounds, and clicks. Dolphins communicate with whistle-like sounds produced by vibrating connective tissue, similar to the way human vocal cords function,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and through burst-pulsed sounds, though the nature and extent of that ability is not known. The clicks are directional and are for echolocation, often occurring in a short series called a click train. The click rate increases when approaching an object of interest. Dolphin echolocation clicks are amongst the <a href="/wiki/Whale_sound" class="mw-redirect" title="Whale sound">loudest sounds made by marine animals</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Au2012&#95;119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Au2012-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bottlenose dolphins have been found to have signature whistles, a whistle that is unique to a specific individual. These whistles are used in order for dolphins to communicate with one another by identifying an individual. It can be seen as the dolphin equivalent of a name for humans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These signature whistles are developed during a dolphin's first year; it continues to maintain the same sound throughout its lifetime.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In order to obtain each individual whistle sound, dolphins undergo vocal production learning. This consists of an experience with other dolphins that modifies the signal structure of an existing whistle sound. An auditory experience influences the whistle development of each dolphin. Dolphins are able to communicate to one another by addressing another dolphin through mimicking their whistle. The signature whistle of a male bottlenose dolphin tends to be similar to that of his mother, while the signature whistle of a female bottlenose dolphin tends to be more distinguishing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bottlenose dolphins have a strong memory when it comes to these signature whistles, as they are able to relate to a signature whistle of an individual they have not encountered for over twenty years.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Research done on signature whistle usage by other dolphin species is relatively limited. The research on other species done so far has yielded varied outcomes and inconclusive results.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Because dolphins generally associate in groups, communication is necessary. Signal masking is when other similar sounds (conspecific sounds) interfere with the original acoustic sound.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In larger groups, individual whistle sounds are less prominent. Dolphins tend to travel in pods, upon which there are groups of dolphins that range from a few to many. Although they are traveling in these pods, the dolphins do not necessarily swim right next to each other. Rather, they swim within the same general vicinity. In order to prevent losing one of their pod members, there are higher whistle rates. Because their group members were spread out, this was done in order to continue traveling together.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Jumping_and_playing">Jumping and playing</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:PacificWhiteSidedDolphine.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/PacificWhiteSidedDolphine.jpg/250px-PacificWhiteSidedDolphine.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="155" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/PacificWhiteSidedDolphine.jpg/500px-PacificWhiteSidedDolphine.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1760" data-file-height="1092" /></a><figcaption>Pacific white-sided dolphins porpoising.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Dolphins frequently leap above the water surface, this being done for various reasons. When travelling, jumping can save the dolphin energy as there is less friction while in the air.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Weihs&#95;129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weihs-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This type of travel is known as porpoising.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Weihs&#95;129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weihs-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other reasons include orientation, social displays, fighting, <a href="/wiki/Non-verbal_communication" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-verbal communication">non-verbal communication</a>, entertainment and attempting to dislodge <a href="/wiki/Parasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Parasite">parasites</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dolphins show various types of playful behavior, often including objects, self-made <a href="/wiki/Bubble_ring" title="Bubble ring">bubble rings</a>, other dolphins or other animals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Walker2007&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker2007-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-dolphinplay&#95;132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dolphinplay-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When playing with objects or small animals, common behavior includes carrying the object or animal along using various parts of the body, passing it along to other members of the group or taking it from another member, or throwing it out of the water.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dolphinplay&#95;132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dolphinplay-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dolphins have also been observed harassing animals in other ways, for example by dragging birds underwater without showing any intent to eat them.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dolphinplay&#95;132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dolphinplay-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Playful behaviour that involves another animal species with active participation of the other animal has also been observed. Playful dolphin interactions with humans are the most obvious examples, followed by those with <a href="/wiki/Humpback_whale" title="Humpback whale">humpback whales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Juvenile dolphins off the coast of Western Australia have been observed chasing, capturing, and chewing on <a href="/wiki/Blowfish" class="mw-redirect" title="Blowfish">blowfish</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While some reports state that the dolphins are becoming intoxicated on the <a href="/wiki/Tetrodotoxin" title="Tetrodotoxin">tetrodotoxin</a> in the fishes' skin,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> other reports have characterized this behavior as the normal curiosity and exploration of their environment in which dolphins engage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Tail-walking">Tail-walking</h3></div>
<p>Although this behaviour is highly unusual in wild dolphins, several <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific_bottlenose_dolphin" title="Indo-Pacific bottlenose dolphin">Indo-Pacific bottlenose dolphins</a> (<i>Tursiops aduncus</i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Port_River" title="Port River">Port River</a>, north of <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a>, have been seen to have exhibit "tail-walking".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dd2018&#95;139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dd2018-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-study2018&#95;140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-study2018-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This activity mimicks a standing posture, using the tail to run backwards along the water.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mess2018&#95;141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mess2018-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To perform this movement, the dolphin "forces the majority of its body vertically out of the water and maintains the position by vigorously pumping its tail".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-study2018&#95;140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-study2018-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This started in 1988 when a female named Billie was rescued after becoming trapped in a polluted <a href="/wiki/Marina" title="Marina">marina</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sutton&#95;2022&#95;142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sutton_2022-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and spent two weeks recuperating with captive dolphins. Billie had previously been observed swimming and frolicking with <a href="/wiki/Racehorse" class="mw-redirect" title="Racehorse">racehorses</a> exercising in the Port River in the 1980s. After becoming trapped in a reedy estuary further down the coast, she was rescued and placed with several captive dolphins at a marine park to recuperate. There she observed the captive dolphins performing tail-walking.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mess2018&#95;141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mess2018-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After being returned to the Port River, she continued to perform this trick, and another dolphin, Wave, copied her. Wave, a very active tail-walker, passed on the skill to her daughters, Ripple and Tallula.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dd2018&#95;139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dd2018-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>After Billie's premature death, Wave started tail-walking much more frequently, and other dolphins in the group were observed also performing the behaviour.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-study2018&#95;140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-study2018-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2011, up to 12 dolphins were observed tail-walking, but only females appeared to learn the skill.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-gentside&#95;143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gentside-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In October 2021, a dolphin was observed tail-walking over a number of hours.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sutton&#95;2022&#95;142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sutton_2022-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>
Scientists have found the spread of this behaviour, through up to two generations, surprising, as it brings no apparent advantage, and is very energy-consuming.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-gentside&#95;143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gentside-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2018 study by M. Bossley et al. suggested:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-study2018&#95;140-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-study2018-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Social learning is the most likely mechanism for the introduction and spread of this unusual behaviour, which has no known adaptive function. These observations demonstrate the potential strength of the capacity for spontaneous imitation in bottlenose dolphins, and help explain the origin and spread of foraging specializations observed in multiple populations of this genus.</p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Threats">Threats</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bottle-nosed-dolphin-Lobomycosis-disease.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Bottle-nosed-dolphin-Lobomycosis-disease.jpg/250px-Bottle-nosed-dolphin-Lobomycosis-disease.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Bottle-nosed-dolphin-Lobomycosis-disease.jpg/500px-Bottle-nosed-dolphin-Lobomycosis-disease.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067" /></a><figcaption>Lesions in the <a href="/wiki/Dorsum_(anatomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dorsum (anatomy)">dorsal</a> fin of a bottlenose dolphin caused by <a href="/wiki/Lobomycosis" title="Lobomycosis">lobomycosis</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Mycosis" class="mw-redirect" title="Mycosis">fungal infection</a> of the skin.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Dolphins have few marine enemies. Some species or specific populations have none, making them <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predators</a>. For most of the smaller species of dolphins, only a few of the larger sharks, such as the <a href="/wiki/Bull_shark" title="Bull shark">bull shark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dusky_shark" title="Dusky shark">dusky shark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tiger_shark" title="Tiger shark">tiger shark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Great_white_shark" title="Great white shark">great white shark</a>, are a potential risk, especially for calves.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some of the larger dolphin species, especially <a href="/wiki/Orcas" class="mw-redirect" title="Orcas">orcas</a>, may also prey on smaller dolphins, but this seems rare.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dolphins also suffer from a wide variety of diseases and parasites.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Cetacean_morbillivirus" title="Cetacean morbillivirus">Cetacean morbillivirus</a> in particular has been known to cause regional <a href="/wiki/Epizootics" class="mw-redirect" title="Epizootics">epizootics</a> often leaving hundreds of animals of various species dead.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Symptoms of infection are often a severe combination of <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">encephalitis</a> and damage to the immune system, which greatly impair the cetacean's ability to swim and stay afloat unassisted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mass&#95;150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mass-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-austurs&#95;151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-austurs-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A study at the U.S. National Marine Mammal Foundation revealed that dolphins, like humans, develop a natural form of <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" class="mw-redirect" title="Diabetes mellitus">type 2 diabetes</a> which may lead to a better understanding of the disease and new treatments for both humans and dolphins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dolphins can tolerate and recover from extreme injuries such as <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">shark</a> bites although the exact methods used to achieve this are not known. The <a href="/wiki/Healing" title="Healing">healing</a> process is rapid and even very deep wounds do not cause dolphins to <a href="/wiki/Hemorrhage" class="mw-redirect" title="Hemorrhage">hemorrhage</a> to death. Furthermore, even gaping wounds restore in such a way that the animal's body shape is restored, and infection of such large wounds seems rare.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A study published in the journal <i>Marine Mammal Science</i> suggests that at least some dolphins survive shark attacks using everything from sophisticated combat moves to teaming up against the shark.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Humans">Humans</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Dolphin_drive_hunting" title="Dolphin drive hunting">Dolphin drive hunting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cetacean_bycatch" title="Cetacean bycatch">Cetacean bycatch</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Rows of dead dolphin lying on concrete" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands.jpg/250px-Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands.jpg/500px-Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1704" /></a><figcaption>Dead Atlantic white-sided dolphins in <a href="/wiki/Hvalba" title="Hvalba">Hvalba</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, killed in a <a href="/wiki/Dolphin_drive_hunting" title="Dolphin drive hunting">drive hunt</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Some dolphin species are at risk of extinction, especially some river dolphin species such as the Amazon river dolphin, and the <a href="/wiki/Ganges_river_dolphin" title="Ganges river dolphin">Ganges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baiji" title="Baiji">Yangtze river dolphin</a>, which are critically or seriously endangered. A 2006 survey found no individuals of the Yangtze river dolphin. The species now appears to be <a href="/wiki/Functionally_extinct" class="mw-redirect" title="Functionally extinct">functionally extinct</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pesticides" class="mw-redirect" title="Pesticides">Pesticides</a>, heavy metals, plastics, and other industrial and agricultural pollutants that do not disintegrate rapidly in the environment concentrate in predators such as dolphins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Injuries or deaths due to collisions with boats, especially their <a href="/wiki/Propeller" title="Propeller">propellers</a>, are also common.
</p><p>Various fishing methods, most notably purse <a href="/wiki/Seine_fishing" title="Seine fishing">seine fishing</a> for <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a> and the use of <a href="/wiki/Drift_net" class="mw-redirect" title="Drift net">drift</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gill_net" class="mw-redirect" title="Gill net">gill nets</a>, unintentionally kill many dolphins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Clover2008&#95;159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clover2008-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Accidental <a href="/wiki/By-catch" class="mw-redirect" title="By-catch">by-catch</a> in gill nets and incidental captures in antipredator nets that protect marine fish farms are common and pose a risk for mainly local dolphin populations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Díaz&#95;López,&#95;2006&#95;160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Díaz_López,_2006-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Díaz&#95;López,&#95;2006b&#95;161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Díaz_López,_2006b-161"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>161<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In some parts of the world, such as <a href="/wiki/Taiji,_Wakayama" title="Taiji, Wakayama">Taiji</a> in Japan and the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, dolphins are traditionally considered food and are killed in <a href="/wiki/Harpoon" title="Harpoon">harpoon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dolphin_drive_hunting" title="Dolphin drive hunting">drive hunts</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-162"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dolphin meat is high in mercury and may thus pose a health danger to humans when consumed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>163<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland's</a> <a href="/wiki/Shark_culling" title="Shark culling">shark culling</a> program, which has killed roughly 50,000 sharks since 1962, has also killed thousands of dolphins as bycatch.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>164<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-afd&#95;cull&#95;165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afd_cull-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "Shark control" programs in both Queensland and <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a> use <a href="/wiki/Shark_nets" class="mw-redirect" title="Shark nets">shark nets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drum_line_(shark_control)" title="Drum line (shark control)">drum lines</a>, which entangle and kill dolphins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>166<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Queensland's "shark control" program has killed more than 1,000 dolphins in recent years,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-afd&#95;cull&#95;165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afd_cull-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and at least 32 dolphins have been killed in Queensland since 2014.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>167<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Shark_culling" title="Shark culling">shark culling</a> program in <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu-Natal" title="KwaZulu-Natal">KwaZulu-Natal</a> has killed at least 2,310 dolphins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>168<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dolphin_safe_label" title="Dolphin safe label">Dolphin safe labels</a> attempt to reassure consumers that fish and other marine products have been caught in a dolphin-friendly way. The earliest campaigns with "dolphin safe" labels were initiated in the 1980s as a result of cooperation between marine activists and the major tuna companies, and involved decreasing incidental dolphin kills by up to 50% by changing the type of nets used to catch tuna. The dolphins are netted only while fishermen are in pursuit of smaller tuna. Albacore are not netted this way, making albacore the only truly dolphin-safe tuna.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>169<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Loud underwater noises, such as those resulting from <a href="/wiki/Navy" title="Navy">naval</a> <a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">sonar</a> use, live firing exercises, and certain <a href="/wiki/Offshore_construction" title="Offshore construction">offshore construction</a> projects such as <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farms</a>, may be harmful to dolphins, increasing stress, damaging hearing, and causing <a href="/wiki/Decompression_sickness" title="Decompression sickness">decompression sickness</a> by forcing them to surface too quickly to escape the noise.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>170<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dolphins and other smaller cetaceans are also hunted in an activity known as dolphin drive hunting. This is accomplished by driving a pod together with boats and usually into a bay or onto a beach. Their escape is prevented by closing off the route to the ocean with other boats or nets. Dolphins are hunted this way in several places around the world, including the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">Solomon Islands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, and Japan, the most well-known practitioner of this method. By numbers, dolphins are mostly hunted for their <a href="/wiki/Whale_meat" title="Whale meat">meat</a>, though some end up in <a href="/wiki/Dolphinarium" title="Dolphinarium">dolphinariums</a>. Despite the controversial nature of the hunt resulting in international criticism, and the possible health risk that the often polluted meat causes, thousands of dolphins are caught in drive hunts each year.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Impacts_of_climate_change">Impacts of climate change</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_oceans" title="Effects of climate change on oceans">Effects of climate change on oceans</a></div>
<p>Dolphins are marine mammals with broad geographic extent, making them susceptible to climate change in various ways. The most common effect of climate change on dolphins is the increasing water temperatures across the globe.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Evans-2013-2&#95;172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-2013-2-172"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This has caused a large variety of dolphin species to experience range shifts, in which the species move from their typical geographic region to cooler waters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Würsig-2001&#95;173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Würsig-2001-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto1&#95;174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-174"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another side effect of increasing water temperatures is the increase in <a href="/wiki/Harmful_algal_bloom" title="Harmful algal bloom">harmful algae blooms</a>, which has caused a mass die-off of bottlenose dolphins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Evans-2013-2&#95;172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-2013-2-172"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title="&#160;Dead link tagged April 2026">dead link</span></a></i>&#93;</span></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, the 1982–83 <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" class="mw-redirect" title="El Niño">El Niño</a> warming event caused the near-bottom spawning <a href="/wiki/Market_squid" class="mw-redirect" title="Market squid">market squid</a> to leave southern California, which caused their predator, the <a href="/wiki/Pilot_whale" title="Pilot whale">pilot whale</a>, to also leave. As the market squid returned six years later, <a href="/wiki/Risso%27s_dolphin" title="Risso&#39;s dolphin">Risso's dolphins</a> came to feed on the squid. <a href="/wiki/Bottlenose_dolphin" title="Bottlenose dolphin">Bottlenose dolphins</a> expanded their range from southern to central California, and stayed even after the warming event subsided.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Würsig-2001&#95;173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Würsig-2001-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pacific_white-sided_dolphin" title="Pacific white-sided dolphin">Pacific white-sided dolphin</a> has had a decline in population in the southwest <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_California" title="Gulf of California">Gulf of California</a>, the southern boundary of their distribution. In the 1980s they were abundant with group sizes up to 200 across the entire cool season. Then, in the 2000s, only two groups were recorded with sizes of 20 and 30, and only across the central cool season. This decline was not related to a decline of other marine mammals or prey, so it was concluded to have been caused by climate change as it occurred during a period of warming. Additionally, the Pacific white-sided dolphin had an increase in occurrence on the west coast of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> from 1984 to 1998.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto1&#95;174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-174"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature" title="Sea surface temperature">sea surface temperatures</a> have increased, as well as <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a> intensity, and sea levels. Because of this, prey resources have been reduced causing a steep decline in the <a href="/wiki/Short-beaked_common_dolphin" class="mw-redirect" title="Short-beaked common dolphin">short-beaked common dolphin</a> Mediterranean subpopulation, which was deemed endangered in 2003. This species now only exists in the <a href="/wiki/Alboran_Sea" title="Alboran Sea">Alboran Sea</a>, due to its high productivity, distinct ecosystem, and differing conditions from the rest of the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>175<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In northwest Europe, many dolphin species have experienced range shifts from the region's typically colder waters. Warm water dolphins, like the short-beaked common dolphin and <a href="/wiki/Striped_dolphin" title="Striped dolphin">striped dolphin</a>, have expanded north of western Britain and into the northern <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, even in the winter, which may displace the <a href="/wiki/White-beaked_dolphin" title="White-beaked dolphin">white-beaked</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_white-sided_dolphin" title="Atlantic white-sided dolphin">Atlantic white-sided dolphin</a> that are in that region. The white-beaked dolphin has shown an increase in the southern North Sea since the 1960s because of this. The <a href="/wiki/Rough-toothed_dolphin" title="Rough-toothed dolphin">rough-toothed dolphin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_spotted_dolphin" title="Atlantic spotted dolphin">Atlantic spotted dolphin</a> may move to northwest Europe.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Evans-2013-2&#95;172-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-2013-2-172"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In northwest <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, white-beaked dolphins (local to the colder waters of the North Atlantic) have decreased while common dolphins (local to warmer waters) have increased from 1992 to 2003.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>176<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Additionally, <a href="/wiki/Fraser%27s_dolphin" title="Fraser&#39;s dolphin">Fraser's dolphin</a>, found in tropical waters, was recorded in the UK for the first time in 1996.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Evans-2013-2&#95;172-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-2013-2-172"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/River_dolphin" title="River dolphin">River dolphins</a> are highly affected by climate change as high evaporation rates, increased water temperatures, decreased precipitation, and increased <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">acidification</a> occur.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Würsig-2001&#95;173-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Würsig-2001-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gomez-Salazar-2012&#95;177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez-Salazar-2012-177"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> River dolphins typically have a higher densities when rivers have a lox index of freshwater degradation and better water quality. <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (April 2025)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gomez-Salazar-2012&#95;177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez-Salazar-2012-177"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Specifically looking at the <a href="/wiki/Ganges_river_dolphin" title="Ganges river dolphin">Ganges river dolphin</a>, the high evaporation rates and increased flooding on the plains may lead to more human river regulation, decreasing the dolphin population.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Würsig-2001&#95;173-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Würsig-2001-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As warmer waters lead to a decrease in dolphin prey, this led to other causes of dolphin population decrease. In the case of bottlenose dolphins, <a href="/wiki/Mullet_(fish)" title="Mullet (fish)">mullet</a> populations decrease due to increasing water temperatures, which leads to a decrease in the dolphins' health and thus their population.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Würsig-2001&#95;173-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Würsig-2001-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At the Shark Bay World Heritage Area in Western Australia, the local <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific_bottlenose_dolphin" title="Indo-Pacific bottlenose dolphin">Indo-Pacific bottlenose dolphin</a> population had a significant decline after a marine heatwave in 2011. This heatwave caused a decrease in prey, which led to a decline in dolphin reproductive rates as female dolphins could not get enough nutrients to sustain a calf.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>178<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The resultant decrease in fish population due to warming waters has also influenced humans to see dolphins as fishing competitors or even bait. Humans use <a href="/wiki/Dusky_dolphin" title="Dusky dolphin">dusky dolphins</a> as bait or are killed off because they consume the same fish humans eat and sell for profit.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Würsig-2001&#95;173-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Würsig-2001-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the central Brazilian Amazon alone, approximately 600 <a href="/wiki/Pink_River_Dolphin" class="mw-redirect" title="Pink River Dolphin">pink river dolphins</a> are killed each year to be used as bait.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gomez-Salazar-2012&#95;177-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez-Salazar-2012-177"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Relationships_with_humans">Relationships with humans</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_history_and_religion">In history and religion</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Akrotiri_dolphins.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Akrotiri_dolphins.jpg/250px-Akrotiri_dolphins.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Akrotiri_dolphins.jpg/500px-Akrotiri_dolphins.jpg 2x" data-file-width="580" data-file-height="384" /></a><figcaption>Fresco of dolphins, c. 1600 BC, from <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_stater_from_Tarentum_from_290-280_BC.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Silver_stater_from_Tarentum_from_290-280_BC.jpg/250px-Silver_stater_from_Tarentum_from_290-280_BC.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="117" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Silver_stater_from_Tarentum_from_290-280_BC.jpg/500px-Silver_stater_from_Tarentum_from_290-280_BC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1745" data-file-height="820" /></a><figcaption>Silver stater from <a href="/wiki/Taranto" title="Taranto">Tarentum</a> c. 290 BC showing Phalanthos riding a dolphin on one side and a rider with a shield decorated with a dolphin on the other side</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Vessel_in_form_of_killer_whale_-_Nazca_pottery_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06105.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Vessel_in_form_of_killer_whale_-_Nazca_pottery_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06105.JPG/250px-Vessel_in_form_of_killer_whale_-_Nazca_pottery_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06105.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Vessel_in_form_of_killer_whale_-_Nazca_pottery_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06105.JPG/500px-Vessel_in_form_of_killer_whale_-_Nazca_pottery_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06105.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a><figcaption>Vessel in form of <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nazca_culture" title="Nazca culture">Nazca culture</a>, circa 200 AD. <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a> collections.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Dolphins have long played a role in human culture.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek myths</a>, dolphins were seen invariably as helpers of humankind. Dolphins also seem to have been important to the <a href="/wiki/Minoans" class="mw-redirect" title="Minoans">Minoans</a>, judging by artistic evidence from the ruined palace at <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a>. During the 2009 excavations of a major <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean</a> city at <a href="/wiki/Iklaina" title="Iklaina">Iklaina</a>, a striking fragment of a wall painting came to light, depicting a ship with three human figures and dolphins. Dolphins are common in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, and many coins from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a> have been found which feature a man, a boy or a deity riding on the back of a dolphin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greeks</a> welcomed dolphins; spotting dolphins riding in a ship's wake was considered a good omen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Eyers2012&#95;180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eyers2012-180"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>180<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In both ancient and later art, <a href="/wiki/Cupid" title="Cupid">Cupid</a> is often shown <a href="/wiki/Cupid#Cupid_and_dolphins" title="Cupid">riding a dolphin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Delphinus" title="Delphinus">A dolphin</a> rescued the poet <a href="/wiki/Arion" title="Arion">Arion</a> from drowning and carried him safe to land, at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Matapan" title="Cape Matapan">Cape Matapan</a>, a promontory forming the southernmost point of the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Peloponnesus">Peloponnesus</a>. There was a temple to <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a> and a statue of Arion riding the dolphin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>181<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The Greeks reimagined the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> god <a href="/wiki/Melqart" title="Melqart">Melqart</a> as Melikertês (<a href="/wiki/Melicertes" title="Melicertes">Melicertes</a>) and made him the son of <a href="/wiki/Athamas" title="Athamas">Athamas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ino_(Greek_mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ino (Greek mythology)">Ino</a>. He drowned but was transfigured as the marine deity Palaemon, while his mother became <a href="/wiki/Leucothea" title="Leucothea">Leucothea</a>. (<i>cf</i> <a href="/wiki/Ino_(Greek_mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ino (Greek mythology)">Ino</a>.) At Corinth, he was so closely connected with the cult of Poseidon that the <a href="/wiki/Isthmian_Games" title="Isthmian Games">Isthmian Games</a>, originally instituted in Poseidon's honor, came to be looked upon as the funeral games of <a href="/wiki/Melicertes" title="Melicertes">Melicertes</a>. <a href="/wiki/Phalanthus_of_Tarentum" class="mw-redirect" title="Phalanthus of Tarentum">Phalanthus</a> was another legendary character brought safely to shore (in Italy) on the back of a dolphin, according to <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a> was once captured by <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_civilization" title="Etruscan civilization">Etruscan</a> <a href="/wiki/Pirate" class="mw-redirect" title="Pirate">pirates</a> who mistook him for a wealthy prince they could ransom. After the ship set sail Dionysus invoked his divine powers, causing vines to overgrow the ship where the mast and sails had been. He turned the oars into serpents, so terrifying the sailors that they jumped overboard, but Dionysus took pity on them and <a href="/wiki/Shapeshifting" title="Shapeshifting">transformed</a> them into dolphins so that they would spend their lives providing help for those in need. Dolphins were also the messengers of Poseidon and sometimes did errands for him as well. Dolphins were sacred to both <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>.
</p><p>"Dolfin" was the name of an aristocratic family in the maritime <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a>, whose most prominent member was the 13th-century <a href="/wiki/Doge_of_Venice" title="Doge of Venice">Doge</a> <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Dolfin" title="Giovanni Dolfin">Giovanni Dolfin</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Hindu_mythology" title="Hindu mythology">Hindu mythology</a> the <a href="/wiki/Ganges_river_dolphin" title="Ganges river dolphin">Ganges river dolphin</a> is associated with <a href="/wiki/Ganga_in_Hinduism" class="mw-redirect" title="Ganga in Hinduism">Ganga</a>, the deity of the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a> river. The dolphin is said to be among the creatures which heralded the goddess' descent from the heavens and her mount, the <a href="/wiki/Makara_(Hindu_mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Makara (Hindu mythology)">Makara</a>, is sometimes depicted as a dolphin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Singh1997&#95;182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singh1997-182"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>182<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Boto" title="Boto">Boto</a>, a species of <a href="/wiki/River_dolphin" title="River dolphin">river dolphin</a> that resides in the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">Amazon River</a>, are believed to be shapeshifters, or <i><a href="/wiki/Encantado_(mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Encantado (mythology)">encantados</a></i>, who are capable of having children with human women.
</p><p>There are comparatively few surviving myths of dolphins in <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesian</a> cultures, in spite of their maritime traditions and reverence of other marine animals such as <a href="/wiki/Sharks" class="mw-redirect" title="Sharks">sharks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seabirds" class="mw-redirect" title="Seabirds">seabirds</a>; unlike these, they are more often perceived as food than as totemic symbols. Dolphins are most clearly represented in <a href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui" class="mw-redirect" title="Rapa Nui">Rapa Nui</a> <a href="/wiki/Rongorongo" title="Rongorongo">Rongorongo</a>, and in the traditions of the <a href="/wiki/Caroline_Islands" title="Caroline Islands">Caroline Islands</a> they are depicted similarly to the Boto, being sexually active shapeshifters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>183<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Heraldry">Heraldry</h4></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dolphin_(heraldry)" title="Dolphin (heraldry)">Dolphin (heraldry)</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dauphin_of_Viennois_Arms.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Dauphin_of_Viennois_Arms.svg/250px-Dauphin_of_Viennois_Arms.svg.png" decoding="async" width="190" height="209" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Dauphin_of_Viennois_Arms.svg/500px-Dauphin_of_Viennois_Arms.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="660" /></a><figcaption>Coat of arms of the <a href="/wiki/Dauphin%C3%A9" title="Dauphiné">Dauphiné</a>, France, featuring a stylised heraldic dolphin</figcaption></figure>
<p>Dolphins are also used as symbols, for instance in heraldry. When heraldry developed in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, little was known about the biology of the dolphin and it was often depicted as a sort of fish. The <a href="/wiki/Dolphin_(heraldry)" title="Dolphin (heraldry)">stylised heraldic dolphin</a> still conventionally follows this tradition, sometimes showing the dolphin skin covered with <a href="/wiki/Fish_scale" title="Fish scale">fish scales</a>.
</p><p>A well-known historical example was the coat of arms of the former province of the <a href="/wiki/Dauphin%C3%A9" title="Dauphiné">Dauphiné</a> in southern France, from which were derived the arms and the title of the <a href="/wiki/Dauphin_of_France" title="Dauphin of France">Dauphin of France</a>, the heir to the former throne of France (the title literally meaning "The Dolphin of France").
</p><p>Dolphins are present in the <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Anguilla" title="Coat of arms of Anguilla">coat of arms of Anguilla</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Romania" title="Coat of arms of Romania">coat of arms of Romania</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hentea2007&#95;184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hentea2007-184"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>184<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Barbados" title="Coat of arms of Barbados">coat of arms of Barbados</a> has a dolphin <a href="/wiki/Supporter" title="Supporter">supporter</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>185<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ali1996&#95;186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali1996-186"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>186<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Poole" title="Coat of arms of Poole">coat of arms of the town of Poole</a>, Dorset, England, first recorded in 1563, includes a dolphin, which was historically depicted in stylised heraldic form, but which since 1976 has been depicted naturalistically.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>187<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_captivity">In captivity</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sea_World1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Sea_World1.jpg/250px-Sea_World1.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Sea_World1.jpg/500px-Sea_World1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="575" data-file-height="384" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/SeaWorld" title="SeaWorld">SeaWorld</a> show featuring bottlenose dolphins and pilot whales.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The renewed popularity of dolphins in the 1960s resulted in the appearance of many <a href="/wiki/Dolphinarium" title="Dolphinarium">dolphinaria</a> around the world, making dolphins accessible to the public. Criticism and <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a> laws forced many to close, although hundreds still exist around the world. In the United States, the best known are the <a href="/wiki/SeaWorld" title="SeaWorld">SeaWorld</a> <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal_park" title="Marine mammal park">marine mammal parks</a>.
In the Middle East the best known are <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dolphin_Bay&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dolphin Bay (page does not exist)">Dolphin Bay</a> at <a href="/wiki/Atlantis,_The_Palm" title="Atlantis, The Palm">Atlantis, The Palm</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>188<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Dubai_Dolphinarium" title="Dubai Dolphinarium">Dubai Dolphinarium</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>189<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dolphin_looks_at_Guest_-_SeaWorld_San_Diego.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Dolphin_looks_at_Guest_-_SeaWorld_San_Diego.jpg/250px-Dolphin_looks_at_Guest_-_SeaWorld_San_Diego.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Dolphin_looks_at_Guest_-_SeaWorld_San_Diego.jpg/500px-Dolphin_looks_at_Guest_-_SeaWorld_San_Diego.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4272" data-file-height="2848" /></a><figcaption>A dolphin looks at a guest at <a href="/wiki/SeaWorld_San_Diego" title="SeaWorld San Diego">SeaWorld San Diego</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Various species of dolphins are kept in captivity. These small cetaceans are more often than not kept in theme parks, such as <a href="/wiki/SeaWorld" title="SeaWorld">SeaWorld</a>, commonly known as a <a href="/wiki/Dolphinarium" title="Dolphinarium">dolphinarium</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bottlenose_dolphin" title="Bottlenose dolphin">Bottlenose dolphins</a> are the most common species of dolphin kept in dolphinariums as they are relatively easy to train, have a long lifespan in captivity and have a friendly appearance. Hundreds if not thousands of bottlenose dolphins live in captivity across the world, though exact numbers are hard to determine. Other species kept in captivity are <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_spotted_dolphin" title="Atlantic spotted dolphin">spotted dolphins</a>, <a href="/wiki/False_killer_whale" title="False killer whale">false killer whales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_dolphin" title="Common dolphin">common dolphins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commerson%27s_dolphin" title="Commerson&#39;s dolphin">Commerson's dolphins</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Rough-toothed_dolphin" title="Rough-toothed dolphin">rough-toothed dolphins</a>, but all in much lower numbers than the bottlenose dolphin. There are also fewer than ten <a href="/wiki/Pilot_whale" title="Pilot whale">pilot whales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amazon_river_dolphin" title="Amazon river dolphin">Amazon river dolphins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Risso%27s_dolphin" title="Risso&#39;s dolphin">Risso's dolphins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spinner_dolphin" title="Spinner dolphin">spinner dolphins</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Tucuxi" title="Tucuxi">tucuxi</a> in captivity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>190<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An unusual and very rare <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrid</a> dolphin, known as a <a href="/wiki/Wolphin" class="mw-redirect" title="Wolphin">wolphin</a>, is kept at the Sea Life Park in Hawaii, which is a cross between a bottlenose dolphin and a <a href="/wiki/False_killer_whale" title="False killer whale">false killer whale</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>191<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The number of <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orcas</a> kept in captivity is very small, especially when compared to the number of bottlenose dolphins, with 60 <a href="/wiki/List_of_captive_orcas" title="List of captive orcas">captive orcas</a> being held in aquaria as of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dolphin&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>192<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The orca's <a href="/wiki/Animal_intelligence" class="mw-redirect" title="Animal intelligence">intelligence</a>, trainability, striking appearance, playfulness in captivity and sheer size have made it a popular exhibit at aquaria and aquatic theme parks. From 1976 to 1997, 55 whales were taken from the wild in Iceland, 19 from Japan, and three from Argentina. These figures exclude animals that died during capture. Live captures fell dramatically in the 1990s, and by 1999, about 40% of the 48 animals on display in the world were captive-born.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dolphins&#95;34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dolphins-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/Mote_Marine_Laboratory" title="Mote Marine Laboratory">Mote Marine Laboratory</a> rescue and <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_rehabilitation" title="Wildlife rehabilitation">rehabilitate</a> sick, wounded, stranded or orphaned dolphins while others, such as the <a href="/wiki/Whale_and_Dolphin_Conservation" title="Whale and Dolphin Conservation">Whale and Dolphin Conservation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Dolphin_Conservation_Society" title="Hong Kong Dolphin Conservation Society">Hong Kong Dolphin Conservation Society</a>, work on dolphin conservation and welfare. <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> has declared the dolphin as its national aquatic animal in an attempt to protect the <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered</a> <a href="/wiki/Ganges_river_dolphin" title="Ganges river dolphin">Ganges river dolphin</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Vikramshila_Gangetic_Dolphin_Sanctuary" title="Vikramshila Gangetic Dolphin Sanctuary">Vikramshila Gangetic Dolphin Sanctuary</a> has been created in the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a> river for the protection of the animals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>193<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Military">Military</h4></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_dolphin" class="mw-redirect" title="Military dolphin">Military dolphin</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:NMMP_dolphin_with_locator.jpeg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/NMMP_dolphin_with_locator.jpeg/250px-NMMP_dolphin_with_locator.jpeg" decoding="async" width="190" height="228" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/NMMP_dolphin_with_locator.jpeg/500px-NMMP_dolphin_with_locator.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1659" data-file-height="1994" /></a><figcaption>A military dolphin</figcaption></figure>
<p>A number of militaries have employed dolphins for various purposes from finding mines to rescuing lost or trapped humans. The <a href="/wiki/Military_dolphins" class="mw-redirect" title="Military dolphins">military use of dolphins</a> drew scrutiny during the Vietnam War, when rumors circulated that the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> was training dolphins to kill Vietnamese divers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>194<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The United States Navy denies that at any point dolphins were trained for combat. Dolphins are still being trained by the United States Navy for other tasks as part of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Navy_Marine_Mammal_Program" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Navy Marine Mammal Program">U.S. Navy Marine Mammal Program</a>. The Russian military is believed to have closed its marine mammal program in the early 1990s. In 2000 the press reported that dolphins trained to kill by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Navy" title="Soviet Navy">Soviet Navy</a> had been sold to <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>195<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The military is also interested in disguising underwater communications as artificial dolphin clicks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>196<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Therapy">Therapy</h4></div>
<p>Dolphins are an increasingly popular choice of <a href="/wiki/Animal-assisted_therapy" title="Animal-assisted therapy">animal-assisted therapy</a> for psychological problems and developmental disabilities. For example, a 2005 study found dolphins an effective treatment for mild to moderate depression.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>197<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This study was criticized on several grounds, including a lack of knowledge on whether dolphins are more effective than common pets.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>198<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Review" title="Review">Reviews</a> of this and other published dolphin-assisted therapy (DAT) studies have found important methodological flaws and have concluded that there is no compelling scientific evidence that DAT is a legitimate therapy or that it affords more than fleeting mood improvement.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>199<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Controversy">Controversy</h4></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Captive_orca" class="mw-redirect" title="Captive orca">Captive orca</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:2009-Seaworld-Shamu.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/2009-Seaworld-Shamu.jpg/250px-2009-Seaworld-Shamu.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="159" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/2009-Seaworld-Shamu.jpg/500px-2009-Seaworld-Shamu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="637" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Shamu" title="Shamu">Shamu</a> in 2009, with a collapsed dorsal fin.</figcaption></figure>
<p>There is debate over the welfare of cetaceans in captivity, and often welfare can vary greatly dependent on the levels of care being provided at a particular facility. In the United States, facilities are regularly inspected by federal agencies to ensure that a high standard of welfare is maintained.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Additionally, facilities can apply to become accredited by the Association of Zoos and Aquariums (AZA), which (for accreditation) requires "the highest standards of animal care and welfare in the world" to be achieved. Facilities such as SeaWorld and the Georgia Aquarium are accredited by the AZA. Organizations such as <a href="/wiki/World_Animal_Protection" title="World Animal Protection">World Animal Protection</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Whale_and_Dolphin_Conservation" title="Whale and Dolphin Conservation">Whale and Dolphin Conservation</a> campaign against the practice of keeping them in captivity. In captivity, they often develop pathologies, such as the <a href="/wiki/Dorsal_fin" title="Dorsal fin">dorsal fin</a> collapse seen in 60–90% of male orca. Captives have vastly reduced life expectancies, on average only living into their 20s, although there are examples of orcas living longer, including several over 30 years old, and two captive orcas, Corky II and Lolita, are in their mid-40s. In the wild, females who survive infancy live 46 years on average, and up to 70–80 years in rare cases. Wild males who survive infancy live 31 years on average, and up to 50–60 years.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>200<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Captivity usually bears little resemblance to wild habitat, and captive whales' social groups are foreign to those found in the wild. Critics claim captive life is stressful due to these factors and the requirement to perform circus tricks that are not part of wild orca behavior. Wild orcas may travel up to 160 kilometres (100&#160;mi) in a day, and critics say the animals are too big and intelligent to be suitable for captivity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-cbs2010&#95;201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbs2010-201"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>201<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Captives occasionally act aggressively towards themselves, their tankmates, or humans, which critics say is a result of <a href="/wiki/Stress_(medicine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stress (medicine)">stress</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-armstrong&#95;202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armstrong-202"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>202<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Although dolphins generally interact well with humans, some attacks have occurred, most of them resulting in small injuries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-fishingnj.org&#95;203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fishingnj.org-203"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>203<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">Orcas</a>, the largest species of dolphin, have been involved in fatal <a href="/wiki/Orca_attacks_on_humans" class="mw-redirect" title="Orca attacks on humans">attacks on humans</a> in captivity. The record-holder of documented orca fatal attacks is a male named <a href="/wiki/Tilikum_(orca)" title="Tilikum (orca)">Tilikum</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>204<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>205<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>206<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> who lived at <a href="/wiki/SeaWorld" title="SeaWorld">SeaWorld</a> from 1992 until his death in 2017.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>207<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Tilikum has played a role in the death of three people in three different incidents (1991, 1999 and 2010).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>208<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Tilikum's behaviour sparked the production of the documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Blackfish_(film)" title="Blackfish (film)">Blackfish</a></i>, which focuses on the consequences of keeping orcas in captivity. There are documented incidents in the wild, too, but none of them fatal.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>209<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Fatal attacks from other species are less common, but there is a registered occurrence off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> in 1994, when a man died after being attacked by a <a href="/wiki/Bottlenose_dolphin" title="Bottlenose dolphin">bottlenose dolphin</a> named <a href="/wiki/Ti%C3%A3o_(dolphin)" title="Tião (dolphin)">Tião</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>210<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>211<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ti%C3%A3o_(dolphin)" title="Tião (dolphin)">Tião</a> had suffered harassment by human visitors, including attempts to stick ice cream sticks down his <a href="/wiki/Blowhole_(anatomy)" title="Blowhole (anatomy)">blowhole</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>212<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Non-fatal incidents occur more frequently, both in the wild and in captivity.
</p><p>While dolphin attacks occur far less frequently than attacks by other sea animals, such as <a href="/wiki/Shark_attack" title="Shark attack">sharks</a>, some scientists are worried about the careless programs of human-dolphin interaction. Dr. Andrew J. Read, a biologist at the Duke University Marine Laboratory who studies dolphin attacks, points out that dolphins are large and wild predators, so people should be more careful when they interact with them.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-fishingnj.org&#95;203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fishingnj.org-203"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>203<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Several scientists who have researched dolphin behaviour have proposed that dolphins' unusually high intelligence in comparison to other animals means that dolphins should be seen as <a href="/wiki/Non-human_person" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-human person">non-human persons</a> who should have their own specific rights and that it is <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morally</a> unacceptable to keep them captive for entertainment purposes or to kill them either intentionally for consumption or unintentionally as by-catch.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-White2009&#95;213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White2009-213"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>213<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>214<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Four countries – <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> – have declared dolphins to be "non-human persons" and have banned the capture and import of live dolphins for entertainment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>215<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>216<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>217<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Consumption">Consumption</h3></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Cuisine">Cuisine</h4></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dolphin_Sashimi.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Dolphin_Sashimi.jpg/250px-Dolphin_Sashimi.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Dolphin_Sashimi.jpg/500px-Dolphin_Sashimi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a><figcaption>Plate of dolphin <a href="/wiki/Sashimi" title="Sashimi">sashimi</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>In some parts of the world, such as <a href="/wiki/Taiji,_Wakayama" title="Taiji, Wakayama">Taiji, Japan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, dolphins are traditionally considered as food, and are killed in <a href="/wiki/Harpoon" title="Harpoon">harpoon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dolphin_drive_hunting" title="Dolphin drive hunting">drive hunts</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-162"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Dolphin meat is consumed in a small number of countries worldwide, which include Japan<sup id="cite&#95;ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>218<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> (where it is referred to as <i>chancho marino</i>, or "sea pork").<sup id="cite&#95;ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>219<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While Japan may be the best-known and most controversial example, only a very small minority of the population has ever sampled it.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>220<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dolphin meat is dense and such a dark shade of red as to appear black. Fat is located in a layer of <a href="/wiki/Blubber" title="Blubber">blubber</a> between the meat and the skin. When dolphin meat is eaten in Japan, it is often cut into thin strips and eaten raw as <i><a href="/wiki/Sashimi" title="Sashimi">sashimi</a></i>, garnished with onion and either <a href="/wiki/Horseradish" title="Horseradish">horseradish</a> or grated garlic, much as with <i>sashimi</i> of <a href="/wiki/Whale_meat" title="Whale meat">whale</a> or <a href="/wiki/Horse_meat" title="Horse meat">horse meat</a> (<i>basashi</i>). When cooked, dolphin meat is cut into bite-size cubes and then batter-fried or simmered in a <i><a href="/wiki/Miso" title="Miso">miso</a></i> sauce with vegetables. Cooked dolphin meat has a flavor very similar to <a href="/wiki/Beef_liver" class="mw-redirect" title="Beef liver">beef liver</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>221<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Health_concerns">Health concerns</h4></div>
<p>There have been human health concerns associated with the consumption of dolphin meat in Japan after tests showed that dolphin meat contained high levels of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-WHO&#95;222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-222"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>222<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There are no known cases of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_poisoning" title="Mercury poisoning">mercury poisoning</a> as a result of consuming dolphin meat, though the government continues to monitor people in areas where dolphin meat consumption is high. The Japanese government recommends that children and pregnant women avoid eating dolphin meat on a regular basis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>223<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Similar concerns exist with the consumption of dolphin meat in the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_development" title="Prenatal development">prenatal</a> exposure to <a href="/wiki/Methylmercury" title="Methylmercury">methylmercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyl" title="Polychlorinated biphenyl">PCBs</a> primarily from the consumption of pilot whale meat has resulted in <a href="/wiki/Neuropsychology" title="Neuropsychology">neuropsychological</a> deficits amongst children.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-WHO&#95;222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-222"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>222<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a> population was exposed to <a href="/wiki/Methylmercury" title="Methylmercury">methylmercury</a> largely from contaminated pilot whale meat, which contained very high levels of about 2 mg methylmercury/kg. However, the Faroe Islands populations also eat significant numbers of fish. The study of about 900 Faroese children showed that prenatal exposure to methylmercury resulted in neuropsychological deficits at 7 years of age</p></blockquote><div class="templatequotecite"><p style="display: inline; padding-left: 2.3em;">—&#8202;<a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-WHO&#95;222-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-222"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>222<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><span style="display:none" data-plural="0"></span></p></div>
